<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Thin material for writing, printing, etc.</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Paper_(disambiguation)" title="Paper (disambiguation)">Paper (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

colspan="2">

<p><b>Paper</b> is a thin sheet <a href="/wiki/Material" title="Material">material</a> produced by mechanically or chemically processing <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fibre" title="Fibre">fibres</a> derived from <a href="/wiki/Wood" title="Wood">wood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">rags</a>, <a href="/wiki/Poaceae" title="Poaceae">grasses</a> or other vegetable sources in <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, draining the water through fine mesh leaving the fibre evenly distributed on the surface, followed by pressing and drying. Although paper was originally made in single sheets by hand, almost all is now made on large machines—some making reels 10 metres wide, running at 2,000 metres per minute and up to 600,000 tonnes a year. It is a versatile material with many uses, including <a href="/wiki/Printing" title="Printing">printing</a>, painting, graphics, signage, design, packaging, decorating, <a href="/wiki/Writing" title="Writing">writing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Housekeeping" title="Housekeeping">cleaning</a>. It may also be used as filter paper, wallpaper, book endpaper, conservation paper, laminated worktops, toilet tissue, or currency and security paper, or in a number of industrial and construction processes.
</p><p>The papermaking process developed in east Asia, probably <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, at least as early as 105 CE,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> court <a href="/wiki/Eunuch" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a> <a href="/wiki/Cai_Lun" title="Cai Lun">Cai Lun</a>, although the earliest archaeological fragments of paper derive from the 2nd century BCE in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsien1985_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tsien1985-2">[2]</a></sup> The modern <a href="/wiki/Pulp_and_paper_industry" title="Pulp and paper industry">pulp and paper industry</a> is global, with China leading its production and the United States following.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_paper" title="History of paper">History of paper</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1128" data-file-width="1160" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/220px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/330px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg/440px-Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_hemp_paper_western_han.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">Hemp</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrapping_paper" title="Wrapping paper">wrapping paper</a>, China, c. 100 BC</div></div></div>
<p>The oldest known archaeological fragments of the immediate precursor to modern paper date to the 2nd century BCE in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. The pulp papermaking process is ascribed to <a href="/wiki/Cai_Lun" title="Cai Lun">Cai Lun</a>, a 2nd-century CE <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> court <a href="/wiki/Eunuch" title="Eunuch">eunuch</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsien1985_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tsien1985-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p><p>It has been said that knowledge of papermaking was passed to the Islamic world after the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> in 751 CE when two Chinese papermakers were captured as prisoners. Although the veracity of this story is uncertain, paper started to be made in <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> In the 13th century, the knowledge and uses of paper spread from the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_Europe" title="Medieval Europe">medieval Europe</a>, where the first water-powered <a href="/wiki/Paper_mill" title="Paper mill">paper mills</a> were built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burns_1996,_417f._4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burns_1996,_417f.-4">[4]</a></sup> Because paper was introduced to the West through the city of Baghdad, it was first called <i>bagdatikos</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> In the 19th century, industrialization greatly reduced the cost of manufacturing paper. In 1844, the Canadian inventor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Fenerty" title="Charles Fenerty">Charles Fenerty</a> and the German inventor <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Gottlob_Keller" title="Friedrich Gottlob Keller">Friedrich Gottlob Keller</a> independently developed processes for pulping wood fibres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_sources_of_fibre">Early sources of fibre</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">wood pulp</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deinking" title="Deinking">deinking</a></div>
<p>Before the industrialisation of paper production the most common fibre source was recycled fibres from used textiles, called rags. The rags were from <a href="/wiki/Hemp" title="Hemp">hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linen" title="Linen">linen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PST1_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-PST1-7">[7]</a></sup> A process for removing printing inks from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Recycled_paper" title="Recycled paper">recycled paper</a> was invented by German jurist <a href="/wiki/Justus_Claproth" title="Justus Claproth">Justus Claproth</a> in 1774.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PST1_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-PST1-7">[7]</a></sup> Today this method is called <a href="/wiki/Deinking" title="Deinking">deinking</a>. It was not until the introduction of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wood_pulp" title="Wood pulp">wood pulp</a> in 1843 that paper production was not dependent on recycled materials from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ragpicker" title="Ragpicker">ragpickers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PST1_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-PST1-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">Papyrus</a></div>
<p>The word <i>paper</i> is etymologically derived from <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">papyrus</i></span>, which comes from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Greek</a> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πᾰ́πῡρος</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><i lang="grc-Latn">pápūros</i></span>), the word for the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Cyperus_papyrus" title="Cyperus papyrus">Cyperus papyrus</a></i></span> plant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Papyrus" title="Papyrus">Papyrus</a> is a thick, paper-like material produced from the pith of the <i><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Cyperus papyrus</i></span></i> plant, which was used in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a> and other <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Mediterranean_region" title="History of the Mediterranean region">Mediterranean</a> cultures for <a href="/wiki/History_of_writing" title="History of writing">writing</a> before the introduction of paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> Although the word <i>paper</i> is etymologically derived from <i>papyrus</i>, the two are produced very differently and the development of the first is distinct from the development of the second. Papyrus is a lamination of natural plant fibre, while paper is manufactured from fibres whose properties have been changed by maceration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tsien1985_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-Tsien1985-2">[2]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Papermaking">Papermaking</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">Papermaking</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_pulping">Chemical pulping</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Kraft_process" title="Kraft process">Kraft process</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfite_process" title="Sulfite process">sulfite process</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Soda_pulping" title="Soda pulping">soda pulping</a></div>
<p>To make pulp from wood, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_pulping_process" title="Chemical pulping process">chemical pulping process</a> separates <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> fibre. A cooking liquor is used to dissolve the <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, which is then washed from the <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a>; this preserves the length of the cellulose fibres. Paper made from chemical pulps are also known as <a href="/wiki/Wood-free_paper" title="Wood-free paper">wood-free papers</a> (not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Tree-free_paper" title="Tree-free paper">tree-free paper</a>); this is because they do not contain lignin, which deteriorates over time. The pulp can also be <a href="/wiki/Bleaching_of_wood_pulp" title="Bleaching of wood pulp">bleached</a> to produce white paper, but this consumes 5% of the fibres. Chemical pulping processes are not used to make paper made from cotton, which is already 90% cellulose.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PaperAutofluorescence.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4800" data-file-width="6400" decoding="async" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PaperAutofluorescence.jpg/300px-PaperAutofluorescence.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PaperAutofluorescence.jpg/450px-PaperAutofluorescence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/PaperAutofluorescence.jpg/600px-PaperAutofluorescence.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PaperAutofluorescence.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The microscopic structure of paper: <a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">Micrograph</a> of paper <a href="/wiki/Autofluorescence" title="Autofluorescence">autofluorescing</a> under <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> illumination. The individual fibres in this sample are around 10 <a href="/wiki/Micrometre" title="Micrometre">µm</a> in diameter.</div></div></div>
<p>There are three main chemical pulping processes: the <a href="/wiki/Sulfite_process" title="Sulfite process">sulfite process</a> dates back to the 1840s and was the dominant method before the second world war. The <a href="/wiki/Kraft_process" title="Kraft process">kraft process</a>, invented in the 1870s and first used in the 1890s, is now the most commonly practised strategy; one of its advantages is the chemical reaction with lignin produces heat, which can be used to run a generator. Most pulping operations using the kraft process are net contributors to the electricity grid or use the electricity to run an adjacent paper mill. Another advantage is that this process recovers and reuses all inorganic chemical reagents. <a href="/wiki/Soda_pulping" title="Soda pulping">Soda pulping</a> is another specialty process used to pulp <a href="/wiki/Straw" title="Straw">straws</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bagasse" title="Bagasse">bagasse</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardwoods" title="Hardwoods">hardwoods</a> with high <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicate</a> content.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanical_pulping">Mechanical pulping</span></h3>
<p>There are two major mechanical pulps: thermomechanical pulp (TMP) and groundwood pulp (GW). In the TMP process, wood is chipped and then fed into steam-heated refiners, where the chips are squeezed and converted to fibres between two steel discs. In the groundwood process, debarked logs are fed into grinders where they are pressed against rotating stones to be made into fibres. Mechanical pulping does not remove the <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, so the yield is very high, &gt; 95%; however, lignin causes the paper thus produced to turn yellow and become brittle over time. Mechanical pulps have rather short fibres, thus producing weak paper. Although large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Electrical_energy" title="Electrical energy">electrical energy</a> are required to produce mechanical pulp, it costs less than the chemical kind.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="De-inked_pulp">De-inked pulp</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Paper_recycling" title="Paper recycling">Paper recycling</a> processes can use either chemically or mechanically produced pulp; by mixing it with water and applying mechanical action the <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> bonds in the paper can be broken and fibres separated again. Most recycled paper contains a proportion of virgin fibre for the sake of quality; generally speaking, de-inked pulp is of the same quality or lower than the collected paper it was made from.
</p><p>There are three main classifications of recycled fibre:
</p>
<ul><li>Mill broke or internal mill waste – This incorporates any substandard or grade-change paper made within the paper mill itself, which then goes back into the manufacturing system to be re-pulped back into paper. Such out-of-specification paper is not sold and is therefore often not classified as genuine reclaimed recycled fibre; however most paper mills have been reusing their own waste fibre for many years, long before recycling became popular.</li>
<li>Preconsumer waste – This is offcut and processing waste, such as guillotine trims and envelope blank waste; it is generated outside the paper mill and could potentially go to landfill, and is a genuine recycled fibre source; it includes de-inked preconsumer waste (recycled material that has been printed but did not reach its intended end use, such as waste from printers and unsold publications).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>Postconsumer waste – This is fibre from paper that has been used for its intended end use and includes office waste, magazine papers and newsprint. As the vast majority of this material has been printed – either digitally or by more conventional means such as lithography or rotogravure – it will either be recycled as printed paper or go through a de-inking process first.</li></ul>
<p>Recycled papers can be made from 100% recycled materials or blended with virgin pulp, although they are (generally) not as strong nor as bright as papers made from the latter.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Additives">Additives</span></h3>
<p>Besides the fibres, pulps may contain fillers such as <a href="/wiki/Chalk" title="Chalk">chalk</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_clay" title="China clay">china clay</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> which improve its characteristics for printing or writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Additives for <a href="/wiki/Sizing" title="Sizing">sizing</a> purposes may be mixed with it or applied to the paper web later in the manufacturing process; the purpose of such sizing is to establish the correct level of surface absorbency to suit ink or paint.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Producing_paper">Producing paper</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Paper_machine" title="Paper machine">Paper machine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3072" data-file-width="4608" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG/220px-M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG/330px-M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG/440px-M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C3%A4nt%C3%A4n_paperitehdas_1.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paper mill in <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A4ntt%C3%A4-Vilppula" title="Mänttä-Vilppula">Mänttä-Vilppula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Pulp_(paper)" title="Pulp (paper)">pulp</a> is fed to a paper machine, where it is formed as a paper web and the water is removed from it by pressing and drying.
</p><p>Pressing the sheet removes the water by force. Once the water is forced from the sheet, a special kind of felt, which is not to be confused with the traditional one, is used to collect the water.  When making paper by hand, a blotter sheet is used instead.
</p><p>Drying involves using air or heat to remove water from the paper sheets. In the earliest days of papermaking, this was done by hanging the sheets like laundry; in more modern times, various forms of heated drying mechanisms are used. On the paper machine, the most common is the steam-heated can dryer. These can reach temperatures above 200 °F (93 °C) and are used in long sequences of more than forty cans where the heat produced by these can easily dry the paper to less than six percent moisture.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Finishing">Finishing</span></h3>
<p>The paper may then undergo <a href="/wiki/Sizing" title="Sizing">sizing</a> to alter its physical properties for use in various applications.
</p><p>Paper at this point is <i>uncoated</i>. <a href="/wiki/Coated_paper" title="Coated paper">Coated paper</a> has a thin layer of material such as <a href="/wiki/Calcium_carbonate" title="Calcium carbonate">calcium carbonate</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/China_clay" title="China clay">china clay</a> applied to one or both sides in order to create a surface more suitable for high-resolution <a href="/wiki/Halftone" title="Halftone">halftone</a> screens. (Uncoated papers are rarely suitable for screens above 150 lpi.) Coated or uncoated papers may have their surfaces polished by <a href="/wiki/Calender" title="Calender">calendering</a>. Coated papers are divided into matte, semi-matte or silk, and gloss. Gloss papers give the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Optical_density" title="Optical density">optical density</a> in the printed image.
</p><p>The paper is then fed onto reels if it is to be used on web printing presses, or cut into sheets for other printing processes or other purposes. The fibres in the paper basically run in the machine direction. Sheets are usually cut "long-grain", i.e. with the grain parallel to the longer dimension of the sheet. <a href="/wiki/Continuous_stationery" title="Continuous stationery">Continuous form paper</a> (or continuous stationery) is cut to width with holes punched at the edges, and folded into stacks.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper_grain">Paper grain</span></h4>
<p>All paper produced by paper machines as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fourdrinier_Machine" title="Fourdrinier Machine">Fourdrinier Machine</a> are wove paper, i.e. the wire mesh that transports the web leaves a pattern that has the same density along the paper grain and across the grain. Textured finishes, <a href="/wiki/Watermark" title="Watermark">watermarks</a> and wire patterns imitating hand-made <i>laid</i> paper can be created by the use of appropriate rollers in the later stages of the machine.
</p><p>Wove paper does not exhibit "laidlines", which are small regular lines left behind on paper when it was handmade in a mould made from rows of metal wires or bamboo. Laidlines are very close together. They run perpendicular to the "chainlines", which are further apart. Handmade paper similarly exhibits "deckle edges", or rough and feathery borders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Applications">Applications</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Money_poster.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Money_poster.JPG/220px-Money_poster.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Money_poster.JPG/330px-Money_poster.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Money_poster.JPG/440px-Money_poster.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Money_poster.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Paper money from different countries</div></div></div>
<p>Paper can be produced with a wide variety of properties, depending on its intended use.
</p>
<h3><span id="Published.2C_written.2C_or_informational_items"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Published,_written,_or_informational_items">Published, written, or informational items</span></h3>
<ul><li><b>For representing value</b>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_money" title="Paper money">paper money</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_note" title="Bank note">bank note</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cheque" title="Cheque">cheque</a>, <a href="/wiki/Security" title="Security">security</a> (see <i><a href="/wiki/Security_paper" title="Security paper">security paper</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Voucher" title="Voucher">voucher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ticket_(admission)" title="Ticket (admission)">ticket</a></li>
<li><b>For <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Data_storage_device" title="Data storage device">storing information</a></b>: <a href="/wiki/Book" title="Book">book</a>, <a href="/wiki/Notebook" title="Notebook">notebook</a>, <a href="/wiki/Graph_paper" title="Graph paper">graph paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punched_card" title="Punched card">punched card</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photographic_paper" title="Photographic paper">photographic paper</a></li>
<li><b>For published materials, publications, and reading materials:</b> books, newspapers, magazines, posters, pamphlets, maps, signs, labels, advertisements, billboards.</li>
<li><b>For individual use</b>: <a href="/wiki/Diary" title="Diary">diary</a>, notebooks, writing pads, memo pads journals, planners, note to remind oneself, etc.; for temporary personal use: scratch paper</li>
<li><b>For business and professional use:</b> copier paper, ledger paper, typing paper, computer printer paper. Specialized paper for forms and documents such as invoices, receipts, tickets, vouchers, bills, contracts, official forms, agreements.</li>
<li><b>For <a href="/wiki/Communication" title="Communication">communication</a></b>: between individuals and/or groups of people:  <a href="/wiki/Letter_(message)" title="Letter (message)">letter</a>, post cards, airmail, telegrams, <a href="/wiki/Newsprint" title="Newsprint">newsprint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Card_stock" title="Card stock">card stock</a></li>
<li><b>For organizing and sending documents</b>: envelopes, file folders, packaging, pocket folders, partition folders.</li>
<li>For artistic works and uses; drawing paper, pastels, water color paintings, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Sketch_pads&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Sketch pads (page does not exist)">sketch pads</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Charcoal_drawings&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Charcoal drawings (page does not exist)">charcoal drawings</a>,</li>
<li>For special printed items using more elegant forms of paper; stationery, parchment,</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Packaging_and_industrial_uses">Packaging and industrial uses</span></h3>
<ul><li><b>For packaging</b>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corrugated_box" title="Corrugated box">corrugated box</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_bag" title="Paper bag">paper bag</a>, <a href="/wiki/Envelope" title="Envelope">envelope</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wrapping_paper" title="Wrapping paper">wrapping paper</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Paper_string&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Paper string (page does not exist)">paper string</a></li>
<li><b>For cleaning</b>: <a href="/wiki/Toilet_paper" title="Toilet paper">toilet paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_towel" title="Paper towel">paper towels</a>, <a href="/wiki/Facial_tissue" title="Facial tissue">facial tissue</a>.</li>
<li><b>For food utensils and containers:</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wax_paper" title="Wax paper">wax paper</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_plate" title="Paper plate">paper plates</a> and paper cups,  <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beverage_cartons" title="Beverage cartons">beverage cartons</a>, tea bags, condiments, food packaging, coffee filters, cupcake cups.</li>
<li><b>For construction</b>: <a href="/wiki/Papier-m%C3%A2ch%C3%A9" title="Papier-mâché">papier-mâché</a>, <a href="/wiki/Origami_paper" title="Origami paper">origami paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_plane" title="Paper plane">paper planes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Quilling" title="Quilling">quilling</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paper_honeycomb" title="Paper honeycomb">paper honeycomb</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sandpaper" title="Sandpaper">sandpaper</a>, used as a core material in <a href="/wiki/Composite_material" title="Composite material">composite materials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_engineering" title="Paper engineering">paper engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Construction_paper" title="Construction paper">construction paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paper_yarn" title="Paper yarn">paper yarn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paper_clothing" title="Paper clothing">paper clothing</a></li>
<li><b>For other uses</b>: <a href="/wiki/Emery_paper" title="Emery paper">emery paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blotting_paper" title="Blotting paper">blotting paper</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Litmus_paper" title="Litmus paper">litmus paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Universal_indicator" title="Universal indicator">universal indicator</a> paper, <a href="/wiki/Paper_chromatography" title="Paper chromatography">paper chromatography</a>, <a href="/wiki/Electrical_insulation_paper" title="Electrical insulation paper">electrical insulation paper</a> (see also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fishpaper" title="Fishpaper">fishpaper</a>), <a href="/wiki/Filter_paper" title="Filter paper">filter paper</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallpaper" title="Wallpaper">wallpaper</a></li></ul>
<p>It is estimated that paper-based storage solutions captured 0.33% of the total in 1986 and only 0.007% in 2007, even though in absolute terms the world's capacity to store information on paper increased from 8.7 to 19.4 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petabyte" title="Petabyte">petabytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HilbertLopez2011_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-HilbertLopez2011-15">[15]</a></sup> It is estimated that in 1986 paper-based postal letters represented less than 0.05% of the world's telecommunication capacity, with sharply decreasing tendency after the massive introduction of digital technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HilbertLopez2011_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-HilbertLopez2011-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paper has a major role in the visual arts. It is used by itself to form two- and three-dimensional shapes and <a href="/wiki/Collage" title="Collage">collages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> It has also evolved to being a structural material used in furniture design.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Watercolor_paper" title="Watercolor paper">Watercolor paper</a> has a long history of production and use.
</p>
<h2><span id="Types.2C_thickness_and_weight"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Types,_thickness_and_weight">Types, thickness and weight</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Paper_size" title="Paper size">Paper size</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammage" title="Grammage">Grammage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Paper_density" title="Paper density">Paper density</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coloured,_textured_craft_card.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2240" data-file-width="3258" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg/220px-Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg/330px-Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg/440px-Coloured%2C_textured_craft_card.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coloured,_textured_craft_card.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Card and paper stock for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crafts" title="Crafts">crafts</a> use comes in a wide variety of textures and colors</div></div></div>
<p>The thickness of paper is often measured by caliper, which is typically given in thousandths of an inch in the United States and in micrometres (µm) in the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Paper may be between 0.07 and 0.18 millimetres (0.0028 and 0.0071 in) thick.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paper is often characterized by weight. In the United States, the weight is the weight of a ream (bundle of 500 sheets) of varying "basic sizes" before the paper is cut into the size it is sold to end customers. For example, a ream of 20 lb, 8.5 in × 11 in (216 mm × 279 mm) paper weighs 5 pounds because it has been cut from larger sheets into four pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> In the United States, printing paper is generally 20 lb, 24 lb, 28 lb, or 32 lb at most. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover_stock" title="Cover stock">Cover stock</a> is generally 68 lb, and 110 lb or more is considered <a href="/wiki/Card_stock" title="Card stock">card stock</a>.
</p><p>In Europe and other regions using the <a href="/wiki/ISO_216" title="ISO 216">ISO 216</a> paper-sizing system, the weight is expressed in grams per square metre (g/m<sup>2</sup> or usually gsm) of the paper. Printing paper is generally between 60 gsm and 120 gsm. Anything heavier than 160 gsm is considered card. The weight of a ream therefore depends on the dimensions of the paper and its thickness.
</p><p>Most commercial paper sold in North America is cut to standard <a href="/wiki/Paper_size" title="Paper size">paper sizes</a> based on <a href="/wiki/United_States_customary_units" title="United States customary units">customary units</a> and is defined by the length and width of a sheet of paper.
</p><p>The ISO 216 system used in most other countries is based on the surface area of a sheet of paper, not on a sheet's width and length. It was first adopted in Germany in 1922 and generally spread as nations adopted the metric system. The largest standard size paper is A0 (A zero), measuring one square metre (approx. 1189 × 841 mm). A1 is half the size of a sheet of A0 (i.e., 594 mm × 841 mm), such that two sheets of A1 placed side by side are equal to one sheet of A0. A2 is half the size of a sheet of A1, and so forth. Common sizes used in the office and the home are A4 and A3 (A3 is the size of two A4 sheets).
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> of paper ranges from 250 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (16 lb/cu ft) for tissue paper to <span style="white-space: nowrap">1<span style="margin-left: 0.25em">500</span></span> kg/m<sup>3</sup> (94 lb/cu ft) for some specialty paper. Printing paper is about 800 kg/m<sup>3</sup> (50 lb/cu ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Paper may be classified into seven categories:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Printing papers</i> of wide variety.</li>
<li><i>Wrapping papers</i> for the protection of goods and merchandise. This includes wax and kraft papers.</li>
<li><i>Writing paper</i> suitable for stationery requirements. This includes ledger, bank, and bond paper.</li>
<li><i>Blotting papers</i> containing little or no size.</li>
<li><i>Drawing papers</i> usually with rough surfaces used by artists and designers, including cartridge paper.</li>
<li><i>Handmade papers</i> including most decorative papers, <a href="/wiki/Ingres_paper" title="Ingres paper">Ingres papers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_paper" title="Japanese paper">Japanese paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tissue_paper" title="Tissue paper">tissues</a>, all characterized by lack of grain direction.</li>
<li><i>Specialty papers</i> including cigarette paper, toilet tissue, and other industrial papers.</li></ul>
<p>Some paper types include:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 22em;">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Bank_paper" title="Bank paper">Bank paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">Banana paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bond_paper" title="Bond paper">Bond paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Book_paper" title="Book paper">Book paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Coated_paper" title="Coated paper">Coated paper</a>: glossy and matte surface</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Construction_paper" title="Construction paper">Construction paper/sugar paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cotton_paper" title="Cotton paper">Cotton paper</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fish_paper" title="Fish paper">Fish paper</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vulcanized" title="Vulcanized">vulcanized</a> fibres for electrical insulation)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Inkjet_paper" title="Inkjet paper">Inkjet paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kraft_paper" title="Kraft paper">Kraft paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Laid_paper" title="Laid paper">Laid paper</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leather_paper" title="Leather paper">Leather paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mummy_paper" title="Mummy paper">Mummy paper</a></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Oak_tag_paper&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Oak tag paper (page does not exist)">Oak tag paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sandpaper" title="Sandpaper">Sandpaper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Troublewit" title="Troublewit">Troublewit</a>, specially pleated paper</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyvek_paper" title="Tyvek paper">Tyvek paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wallpaper" title="Wallpaper">Wallpaper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Washi" title="Washi">Washi</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Waterproof_paper" title="Waterproof paper">Waterproof paper</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wax_paper" title="Wax paper">Wax paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wove_paper" title="Wove paper">Wove paper</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xuan_paper" title="Xuan paper">Xuan paper</a></li></ul>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Paper_stability">Paper stability</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3672" data-file-width="4896" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg/220px-Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg/330px-Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg/440px-Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Book_suffering_from_slow_fire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A book printed in 1920 on <a href="/wiki/Acidic_paper" title="Acidic paper">acidic paper</a>, now disintegrating a hundred years later.</div></div></div>
<p>Much of the early paper made from wood pulp contained significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Alum" title="Alum">alum</a>, a variety of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium_sulfate" title="Aluminium sulfate">aluminium sulfate</a> salt that is significantly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acidic" title="Acidic">acidic</a>. Alum was added to paper to assist in <a href="/wiki/Sizing" title="Sizing">sizing</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bier_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-bier-24">[24]</a></sup> making it somewhat water resistant so that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inks" title="Inks">inks</a> did not "run" or spread uncontrollably. Early papermakers did not realize that the alum they added liberally to cure almost every problem encountered in making their product would be eventually detrimental.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clark_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-clark-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cellulose" title="Cellulose">cellulose</a> fibres that make up paper are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrolyzed" title="Hydrolyzed">hydrolyzed</a> by acid, and the presence of alum eventually degrades the fibres until the <a href="/wiki/Acidic_paper" title="Acidic paper">acidic paper</a> disintegrates in a process known as "<a href="/wiki/Slow_fire" title="Slow fire">slow fire</a>". Documents written on <a href="/wiki/Cotton_paper" title="Cotton paper">rag paper</a> are significantly more stable. The use of non-acidic additives to make paper is becoming more prevalent, and the stability of these papers is less of an issue.
</p><p>Paper made from <a href="/wiki/Pulp_mill#The_mill" title="Pulp mill">mechanical pulp</a> contains significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Lignin" title="Lignin">lignin</a>, a major component in wood. In the presence of light and oxygen, lignin reacts to give yellow materials,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> which is why <a href="/wiki/Newsprint" title="Newsprint">newsprint</a> and other mechanical paper yellows with age. Paper made from <a href="/wiki/Bleaching_of_wood_pulp" title="Bleaching of wood pulp">bleached</a> <a href="/wiki/Kraft_process" title="Kraft process">kraft</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sulfite_process" title="Sulfite process">sulfite</a> pulps does not contain significant amounts of lignin and is therefore better suited for books, documents and other applications where whiteness of the paper is essential.
</p><p>Paper made from wood pulp is not necessarily less durable than a rag paper. The aging behaviour of a paper is determined by its manufacture, not the original source of the fibres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Furthermore, tests sponsored by the Library of Congress prove that all paper is at risk of acid decay, because cellulose itself produces formic, acetic, lactic and oxalic acids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mechanical pulping yields almost a tonne of pulp per tonne of dry wood used, which is why mechanical pulps are sometimes referred to as "high yield" pulps. With almost twice the yield as chemical pulping, mechanical pulps is often cheaper. Mass-market paperback books and newspapers tend to use mechanical papers. Book publishers tend to use <a href="/wiki/Acid-free_paper" title="Acid-free paper">acid-free paper</a>, made from fully bleached chemical pulps for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hardback" title="Hardback">hardback</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_paperbacks" title="Trade paperbacks">trade paperback</a> books.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Environmental_impact_of_paper" title="Environmental impact of paper">Environmental impact of paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a></div>
<p>The production and use of paper has a number of adverse effects on the environment.
</p><p>Worldwide consumption of paper has risen by 400% in the past 40 years<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (November 2017)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> leading to increase in <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a>, with 35% of harvested trees being used for paper manufacture. Most paper companies also plant trees to help regrow forests. Logging of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_growth_forest" title="Old growth forest">old growth forests</a> accounts for less than 10% of wood pulp,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chase_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-chase-29">[29]</a></sup> but is one of the most controversial issues.
</p><p>Paper waste accounts for up to 40% of total waste produced in the United States each year, which adds up to 71.6 million tons of paper waste per year in the United States alone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The average office worker in the US prints 31 pages every day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Americans also use in the order of 16 billion <a href="/wiki/Paper_cup" title="Paper cup">paper cups</a> per year.
</p><p>Conventional bleaching of wood pulp using elemental chlorine produces and releases into the environment large amounts of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Organochloride" title="Organochloride">chlorinated organic compounds</a>, including chlorinated <a href="/wiki/Polychlorinated_dibenzodioxins" title="Polychlorinated dibenzodioxins">dioxins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-can_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-can-32">[32]</a></sup> Dioxins are recognized as a persistent environmental pollutant, regulated internationally by the <a href="/wiki/Stockholm_Convention_on_Persistent_Organic_Pollutants" title="Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants">Stockholm Convention on Persistent Organic Pollutants</a>. Dioxins are highly toxic, and health effects on humans include reproductive, developmental, immune and hormonal problems. They are known to be carcinogenic. Over 90% of human exposure is through food, primarily meat, dairy, fish and shellfish, as dioxins accumulate in the food chain in the fatty tissue of animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>
</p><p>The paper pulp and print industries emitted together about 1% of world <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse-gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse-gas emissions">Greenhouse-gas emissions</a> in 2010<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> and about 0.9% in 2012,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> but less than screens: digital technologies emitted approximately 4% of world <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greenhouse-gas_emissions" title="Greenhouse-gas emissions">Greenhouse-gas emissions</a> in the year 2019 and the number can be two times larger by 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future">Future</span></h2>
<p>Some manufacturers have started using a new, significantly more environmentally friendly alternative to expanded plastic packaging. Made out of paper, and known commercially as PaperFoam, the new packaging has mechanical properties very similar to those of some expanded plastic packaging, but is <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegradable</a> and can also be recycled with ordinary paper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p><p>With increasing environmental concerns about synthetic coatings (such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PFOA" title="PFOA">PFOA</a>) and the higher prices of hydrocarbon based petrochemicals, there is a focus on <a href="/wiki/Zein" title="Zein">zein</a> (corn protein) as a coating for paper in high grease applications such as popcorn bags.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p>Also, synthetics such as <a href="/wiki/Tyvek" title="Tyvek">Tyvek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Teslin_(material)" title="Teslin (material)">Teslin</a> have been introduced as printing media as a more durable material than paper.
</p>
